							<div class="mw-indicators">

					<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-content-subtitle"></div></div>
					
					
					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Archaeological culture</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1257001546">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}@media screen{html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data:not(.notheme)>div:not(.notheme)[style]{background:#1f1f23!important;color:#f8f9fa}}@media screen and (prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data:not(.notheme) div:not(.notheme){background:#1f1f23!important;color:#f8f9fa}}@media(min-width:640px){body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table{display:table!important}body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table>caption{display:table-caption!important}body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table>tbody{display:table-row-group}body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table tr{display:table-row!important}body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table th,body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table td{padding-left:inherit;padding-right:inherit}}</style><table class="infobox"><caption class="infobox-title">Uruk period</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-image"><span class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Frameless"><a href="/wiki/File:Uruk_expansion.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Uruk_expansion.svg/220px-Uruk_expansion.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="178" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Uruk_expansion.svg/330px-Uruk_expansion.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Uruk_expansion.svg/440px-Uruk_expansion.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1185" data-file-height="961" /></a></span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Geographical range</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Period</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Copper Age</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Dates</th><td class="infobox-data">c. 4000–3100 BC</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><a href="/wiki/Type_site" title="Type site">Type site</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Uruk" title="Uruk">Uruk</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Preceded by</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Ubaid_period" title="Ubaid period">Ubaid period</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Followed by</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Jemdet_Nasr_period" title="Jemdet Nasr period">Jemdet Nasr period</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Uruk period</b> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;4000 to 3100 BC</span>; also known as <b>Protoliterate period</b>) existed from the <a href="/wiki/Protohistory" title="Protohistory">protohistoric</a> <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</a> to <a href="/wiki/Early_Bronze_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Bronze Age">Early Bronze Age</a>  period in the history of <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, after the <a href="/wiki/Ubaid_period" title="Ubaid period">Ubaid period</a> and before the <a href="/wiki/Jemdet_Nasr_period" title="Jemdet Nasr period">Jemdet Nasr period</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Named after the Sumerian city of <a href="/wiki/Uruk" title="Uruk">Uruk</a>, this period saw the emergence of urban life in Mesopotamia and the <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian civilization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The late Uruk period (34th to 32nd centuries) saw the gradual emergence of the <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform_script" class="mw-redirect" title="Cuneiform script">cuneiform script</a> and corresponds to the <a href="/wiki/Early_Bronze_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Bronze Age">Early Bronze Age</a>; it has also been described as the "Protoliterate period".<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cdli.ox.ac.uk_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cdli.ox.ac.uk-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>It was during this period that pottery painting declined as copper started to become popular, along with <a href="/wiki/Cylinder_seal" title="Cylinder seal">cylinder seals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Langer_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Langer-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Dating_and_periodization">Dating and periodization</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uruk_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Dating and periodization"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1237032888/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti data-file-width="1582" data-file-height="1100" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">Mesopotamian king as <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Animals" title="Master of Animals">Master of Animals</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Gebel_el-Arak_Knife" title="Gebel el-Arak Knife">Gebel el-Arak Knife</a>, dated circa 3300-3200 BC, <a href="/wiki/Abydos,_Egypt" title="Abydos, Egypt">Abydos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>. This work of art suggests early <a href="/wiki/Egypt-Mesopotamia_relations" class="mw-redirect" title="Egypt-Mesopotamia relations">Egypt-Mesopotamia relations</a>, showing the influence of Mesopotamia on <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> at an early date, and the state of Mesopotamian royal iconography during the Uruk period. <a href="/wiki/Louvre_Museum" class="mw-redirect" title="Louvre Museum">Louvre Museum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:129px;max-width:129px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:178px;overflow:hidden"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Uruk_King-Priest_3300_BCE_portrait_detail.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Uruk_King-Priest_3300_BCE_portrait_detail.jpg/127px-Uruk_King-Priest_3300_BCE_portrait_detail.jpg" decoding="async" width="127" height="179" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Uruk_King-Priest_3300_BCE_portrait_detail.jpg/191px-Uruk_King-Priest_3300_BCE_portrait_detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Uruk_King-Priest_3300_BCE_portrait_detail.jpg/254px-Uruk_King-Priest_3300_BCE_portrait_detail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1304" data-file-height="1833" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">Similar portrait of a probable Uruk King-Priest with a brimmed round hat and large beard, excavated in Uruk and dated to 3300 BC. <a href="/wiki/Louvre_Museum" class="mw-redirect" title="Louvre Museum">Louvre Museum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The term "Uruk period" was coined at a conference in <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a> in 1930, along with the preceding <a href="/wiki/Ubaid_period" title="Ubaid period">Ubaid period</a> and following <a href="/wiki/Jemdet_Nasr_period" title="Jemdet Nasr period">Jemdet Nasr period</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-matthews2002_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-matthews2002-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The chronology of the Uruk period is highly debated and still very uncertain. It is known that it covered most of the 4th millennium BC. But there is no agreement on the date when it began or ended and the major breaks within the period are difficult to determine. This is due primarily to the fact that the original stratigraphy of the central quarter of Uruk is ancient and very unclear and the excavations of it were conducted in the 1930s, before many modern dating techniques existed. These problems are largely linked to the difficulty specialists have had establishing synchronisms between the different archaeological sites and a relative chronology, which would enable the development of a more reliable absolute chronology.
</p><p>The traditional chronology is very imprecise and is based on some key <a href="/wiki/Sondage" title="Sondage">sondages</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Eanna" title="Eanna">Eanna</a> quarter at Uruk.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The most ancient levels of these sondages (XIX–XIII) belong to the end of the Ubaid period (Ubaid V, 4200–3900 or 3700 BC); pottery characteristic of the Uruk period begins to appear in levels XIV/XIII.
</p><p>The Uruk period is traditionally divided into many phases. The first two are "Old Uruk" (levels XII–IX), then "Middle Uruk" (VIII–VI). These first two phases are poorly known, and their chronological limits are poorly defined; many different chronological systems are found in scholarship.
</p><p>From the middle of the 4th millennium, it transitions to the best-known period, "Late Uruk", which continues until around 3200 or 3100 BC. It is in fact in this period that the features which are generally seen as most characteristic of the civilization of the Uruk period occur:<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> high technological development, the development of important urban agglomerations with imposing monumental structures (the most characteristic of these is Level IV of Eanna), the appearance of state institutions, and the expansion of the Uruk civilization throughout the whole Near East.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Jemdet_Nasr_period">Jemdet Nasr period</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uruk_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Jemdet Nasr period"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>This phase of "Late Uruk" is followed by another phase (level III of Eanna) in which the Uruk civilization declined and a number of distinct local cultures developed throughout the Near East.  This is generally known as the <a href="/wiki/Jemdet_Nasr_period" title="Jemdet Nasr period">Jemdet Nasr period</a>, after the archaeological site of that name.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-matthews-55/4-196-203_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-matthews-55/4-196-203-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Its exact nature is highly debated, and it is difficult to clearly distinguish its traits from those of the Uruk culture, so some scholars refer to it as the "Final Uruk" period instead. It lasted from around 3000 to 2950 BC.
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1237032888/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:292px;max-width:292px"><div class="trow"><div class="theader">Uruk King-priest feeding the sacred herd</div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:290px;max-width:290px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:116px;overflow:hidden"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Yale_University._Uruk_period_priest-king.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Yale_University._Uruk_period_priest-king.jpg/288px-Yale_University._Uruk_period_priest-king.jpg" decoding="async" width="288" height="117" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Yale_University._Uruk_period_priest-king.jpg/432px-Yale_University._Uruk_period_priest-king.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Yale_University._Uruk_period_priest-king.jpg/576px-Yale_University._Uruk_period_priest-king.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2071" data-file-height="841" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">The king-priest and his acolyte feeding the sacred herd. Uruk period, ca. 3200 BCE.</div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:290px;max-width:290px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:158px;overflow:hidden"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Cylinder_seal_king_Louvre_AO6620.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Cylinder_seal_king_Louvre_AO6620.jpg/288px-Cylinder_seal_king_Louvre_AO6620.jpg" decoding="async" width="288" height="158" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Cylinder_seal_king_Louvre_AO6620.jpg/432px-Cylinder_seal_king_Louvre_AO6620.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Cylinder_seal_king_Louvre_AO6620.jpg/576px-Cylinder_seal_king_Louvre_AO6620.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1100" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">Cylinder-seal of the Uruk period and its impression, c.3100 BCE. <a href="/wiki/Louvre_Museum" class="mw-redirect" title="Louvre Museum">Louvre Museum</a></div></div></div></div></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Alternative_chronology">Alternative chronology</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uruk_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Alternative chronology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In 2001, a new chronology was proposed by the members of a colloquium at <a href="/wiki/Santa_Fe,_New_Mexico" title="Santa Fe, New Mexico">Santa Fe</a>, based on recent excavations, especially at sites outside Mesopotamia. They consider the Uruk period to be the "Late Chalcolithic" (LC). Their LC 1 corresponds to the end of the Ubaid period and ends around 4200 BC, with the beginning of LC 2, which is the first phase of the Uruk period. They divide "Old Uruk" into two phases, with the dividing line placed around 4000 BC. Around 3800 BC, LC 3 begins, which corresponds to the "Middle Uruk" phase and continues until around 3400 BC, when it is succeeded by LC 4. It rapidly transitions to LC 5 (Late Uruk), which continues until 3000 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Some other chronological proposals have also been put forward, such as by the ARCANE team (Associated Regional Chronologies for the Ancient Near East).<sup id="cite_ref-cdli.ox.ac.uk_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cdli.ox.ac.uk-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Although the chronology of the Uruk period is full of uncertainties, it is generally agreed to have a rough span of a thousand years covering the period from 4000 to 3000 BC and to be divided into several phases: an initial urbanisation and elaboration of Urukian cultural traits marks the transition from the end of the Ubaid period (Old Uruk), then a period of expansion (Middle Uruk), with a peak during which the characteristic traits of the 'Uruk civilization' are definitively established (Late Uruk), and then a retreat of Urukian influence and increase in cultural diversity in the Near East along with a decline of the 'centre'.
</p><p>Some researchers have attempted to explain this final stage as the arrival of new populations of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Semitic-speaking_peoples" title="Ancient Semitic-speaking peoples">Semitic</a> origin (the future <a href="/wiki/Akkadians" class="mw-redirect" title="Akkadians">Akkadians</a>), but there is no conclusive proof of this.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In Lower Mesopotamia, the researchers identify this as the Jemdet Nasr period, which sees a shift to more concentrated habitation, undoubtedly accompanied by a reorganisation of power;<sup id="cite_ref-matthews-55/4-196-203_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-matthews-55/4-196-203-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in southwestern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, it is the <a href="/wiki/Elam#Proto-Elamite_(c._3500_–_c._2700_BC)" title="Elam">Proto-Elamite</a> period; <a href="/wiki/Niniveh" class="mw-redirect" title="Niniveh">Niniveh</a> V in Upper Mesopotamia (which follows the Gawra culture); the "Scarlet Ware" culture in <a href="/wiki/Diyala_Governorate" title="Diyala Governorate">Diyala</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In Lower Mesopotamia, the <a href="/wiki/Early_Dynastic_Period_(Mesopotamia)" title="Early Dynastic Period (Mesopotamia)">Early Dynastic Period</a> begins around the start of the 3rd millennium BC, during which this region again exerts considerable influence over its neighbours.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Lower_Mesopotamia">Lower Mesopotamia</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uruk_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Lower Mesopotamia"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Basse_M%C3%A9sopotamie_Uruk.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Basse_M%C3%A9sopotamie_Uruk.png/330px-Basse_M%C3%A9sopotamie_Uruk.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="295" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/51/Basse_M%C3%A9sopotamie_Uruk.png 1.5x" data-file-width="440" data-file-height="393" /></a><figcaption>Location of the main sites in southern Mesopotamia in the Uruk and Jamdet Nasr periods.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Lower Mesopotamia is the core of the Uruk period culture and the region seems to have been the cultural centre of the time because this is where the principal monuments are found and the most obvious traces of an urban society with state institutions developing in the second half of the 4th millennium BC, the first system of writing, and it is the material and symbolic culture of this region which had the most influence on the rest of the Near East at this time. However, this region is not well-known archaeologically, since only the site of Uruk itself has provided traces of monumental architecture and administrative documents which justify seeing this region as the most dynamic and influential. At some other sites, construction from this period has been found, but they are usually known only as a result of soundages. In the current state of knowledge it remains impossible to determine whether the site of Uruk was actually unique in this region or if it is simply an accident of excavation that makes it seem more important than the others.
</p><p>This is the region of the Near East that was the most <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_ancient_Mesopotamia" class="mw-redirect" title="Agriculture in ancient Mesopotamia">agriculturally</a> productive, as a result of an irrigation system which developed in the 4th millennium BC and focused on the cultivation of <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a> (along with the <a href="/wiki/Date_palm" title="Date palm">date palm</a> and various other fruits and legumes) and the pasturing of <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">sheep</a> for their wool.<sup id="cite_ref-liveco_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-liveco-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Although it lacked mineral resources and was located in an arid area, it had undeniable geographic and environmental advantages: it consisted of a vast <a href="/wiki/River_delta" title="River delta">delta</a>, a flat region transected by waterways, resulting in a potentially vast area of cultivable land, over which communications by river or land were easy.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It may also have become a highly populated and urbanised region in the 4th millennium BC,<sup id="cite_ref-livdemo_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-livdemo-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> with a social hierarchy, artisanal activities, and long-distance commerce. It has been the focus of archaeological investigation led by <a href="/wiki/Robert_McCormick_Adams_Jr." title="Robert McCormick Adams Jr.">Robert McCormick Adams Jr.</a>, whose work has been very important for the understanding of the emergence of urban societies in this region. A clear settlement hierarchy has been identified, dominated by a number of agglomerations which grew more and more important over the 4th millennium BC, of which Uruk seems to have been the most important by far, making this the most ancient known case of <a href="/wiki/Urban_macrocephaly" class="mw-redirect" title="Urban macrocephaly">urban macrocephaly</a>, since its hinterland seems to have reinforced Uruk itself to the detriment of its neighbours (notably the region to the north, around <a href="/wiki/Adab_(city)" title="Adab (city)">Adab</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nippur" title="Nippur">Nippur</a>) in the final part of the period.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The ethnic composition of this region in the Uruk period cannot be determined with certainty. It is connected to the problem of the origins of the <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerians</a> and the dating of their emergence (if they are considered locals of the region) or their arrival (if they are thought to have migrated) in lower Mesopotamia. There is no agreement on the archaeological evidence for a migration, or on whether the earliest form of writing already reflects a specific language. Some argue that it is actually Sumerian, in which case the Sumerians would have been its inventors<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and would have already been present in the region in the final centuries of the 4th millennium at the latest (which seems to be the most widely accepted position).<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Whether other ethnic groups were also present, especially Semitic ancestors of the Akkadians or one or several 'pre-Sumerian' peoples (neither Sumerian nor Semite and predating both in the region) is also debated and cannot be resolved by excavation.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Uruk">Uruk</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uruk_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Uruk"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>

typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:White_Temple_ziggurat_in_Uruk.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/White_Temple_ziggurat_in_Uruk.jpg/288px-White_Temple_ziggurat_in_Uruk.jpg" decoding="async" width="288" height="171" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/White_Temple_ziggurat_in_Uruk.jpg/432px-White_Temple_ziggurat_in_Uruk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/White_Temple_ziggurat_in_Uruk.jpg/576px-White_Temple_ziggurat_in_Uruk.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1952" data-file-height="1158" /></a></span></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Anu/ White Temple ziggurat at Uruk. The original pyramidal structure, the "Anu Ziggurat" dates to around 4000 BCE, and the White Temple was built on top of it circa 3500 BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<p>Out of these urban agglomerations, it is Uruk, the period's <a href="/wiki/Eponymous" class="mw-redirect" title="Eponymous">eponymous</a> site, which was the largest by far, according to our current knowledge, and it is the main one from which the chronological sequence of the period has been constructed. It may have covered 230–500 hectares at its peak during the Late Uruk period, more than the other contemporary large settlements, and it may have had a population of between 25,000 and 50,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The architectural profile of the site consists of two monumental groups located 500 metres apart.
</p><p>The most remarkable constructions are located in the sector called the Eanna (after the temple which was located there in subsequent periods and possibly already at this stage). After the 'Limestone Temple' of level V, a programme of construction hitherto unparalleled was begun in level IV. Thereafter, the buildings were vastly larger than earlier, some had novel designs and new construction techniques were used for the structure and the decoration. Level IV of the Eanna is divided into two monumental groups: in the west, a complex centred on the 'Temple with mosaics' (decorated with mosaics made of painted clay cones) of level IVB, subsequently covered by another building (the 'Riemchen Building') of level IVA. To the east there is a very important group of structures—notably a 'Square Building' and the 'Riemchen Temple Building', which were subsequently replaced by other buildings with original plans, like the 'Hall with Pillars' and the 'Hall with Mosaics', a square 'Grand Court' and two very large buildings with a tripartite plan, 'Temple C' (54 x 22 m) and 'Temple D' (80 x 50 m, the largest building known from the Uruk period).
</p><p>The second monumental sector was attributed to the god <a href="/wiki/Anu" title="Anu">Anu</a> by the excavators of the site, because it was the location of a sanctuary for this god some 3000 years later. It is dominated by a series of temples built on a high terrace after the Ubayd period. The best-preserved of these is the "White Temple" of level IV, which measures 17.5 x 22.3 m and gets its name from white plates that covered its walls. At its base, a building with a labyrinthine plan, called the 'Stone building', was built.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px; height: 150px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Eanna5.svg" class="mw-file-description" title="Eanna, levels VI–V."><img alt="Eanna, levels VI–V." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Eanna5.svg/120px-Eanna5.svg.png" decoding="async" width="120" height="87" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Eanna5.svg/180px-Eanna5.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Eanna5.svg/240px-Eanna5.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1878" data-file-height="1356" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">Eanna, levels VI–V.</div>
		</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px; height: 150px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Eanna4composite.svg" class="mw-file-description" title="Eanna, level IV."><img alt="Eanna, level IV." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Eanna4composite.svg/120px-Eanna4composite.svg.png" decoding="async" width="120" height="87" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Eanna4composite.svg/180px-Eanna4composite.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Eanna4composite.svg/240px-Eanna4composite.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1878" data-file-height="1356" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">Eanna, level IV.</div>
		</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px; height: 150px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Anu_district.svg" class="mw-file-description" title="Sector of An, levels IV–III."><img alt="Sector of An, levels IV–III." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Anu_district.svg/120px-Anu_district.svg.png" decoding="async" width="120" height="87" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Anu_district.svg/180px-Anu_district.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Anu_district.svg/240px-Anu_district.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1878" data-file-height="1357" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">Sector of An, levels IV–III.</div>
		</li>
</ul>
<p>The function of these buildings, which are unparalleled in their size and the fact that they are gathered in monumental groups, is debated. The excavators of the site wanted to see them as 'temples', influenced by the fact that in the historic period, the Eanna was the area dedicated to the goddess <a href="/wiki/Inanna" title="Inanna">Inanna</a> and the other sector was dedicated to the god An. This conformed to the theory of the 'temple-city' which was in vogue during the inter-war period. It is possible that this is actually a place of power formed by a complex of buildings of different forms (palatial residences, administrative spaces, palace chapels), desired by the dominant power in the city, whose nature is still unclear.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In any case, it was necessary to invest considerable effort to construct these buildings, which shows the capacities of the elites of this period. Uruk is also the site of the most important discoveries of early <a href="/wiki/Clay_tablet" title="Clay tablet">writing tablets</a>, in levels IV and III, in a context where they had been disposed of, which means that the context in which they were created is not known to us. Uruk III, which corresponds to the Jemdet Nasr period, sees a complete reorganisation of the Eanna quarter, in which the buildings on the site were razed and replaced by a grand terrace, which ignores the earlier buildings. In their foundations, a deposit which is probably of a cultic nature (the <i>Sammelfund</i>) was found, containing some major artistic works of the period (large cultic vase, cylinder seals, etc.).
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Other_sites_in_Lower_Mesopotamia">Other sites in Lower Mesopotamia</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uruk_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Other sites in Lower Mesopotamia"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Fragment_of_a_bowl_with_a_frieze_of_bulls_in_relief,_ca._3300%E2%80%932900_B.C._Late_Uruk%E2%80%93Jemdet_Nasr._Southern_Mesopotamia.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Fragment_of_a_bowl_with_a_frieze_of_bulls_in_relief%2C_ca._3300%E2%80%932900_B.C._Late_Uruk%E2%80%93Jemdet_Nasr._Southern_Mesopotamia.jpg/220px-Fragment_of_a_bowl_with_a_frieze_of_bulls_in_relief%2C_ca._3300%E2%80%932900_B.C._Late_Uruk%E2%80%93Jemdet_Nasr._Southern_Mesopotamia.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="213" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Fragment_of_a_bowl_with_a_frieze_of_bulls_in_relief%2C_ca._3300%E2%80%932900_B.C._Late_Uruk%E2%80%93Jemdet_Nasr._Southern_Mesopotamia.jpg/330px-Fragment_of_a_bowl_with_a_frieze_of_bulls_in_relief%2C_ca._3300%E2%80%932900_B.C._Late_Uruk%E2%80%93Jemdet_Nasr._Southern_Mesopotamia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Fragment_of_a_bowl_with_a_frieze_of_bulls_in_relief%2C_ca._3300%E2%80%932900_B.C._Late_Uruk%E2%80%93Jemdet_Nasr._Southern_Mesopotamia.jpg/440px-Fragment_of_a_bowl_with_a_frieze_of_bulls_in_relief%2C_ca._3300%E2%80%932900_B.C._Late_Uruk%E2%80%93Jemdet_Nasr._Southern_Mesopotamia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3773" data-file-height="3655" /></a><figcaption>Fragment of a bowl with a frieze of bulls in relief, ca. 3300–2900 B.C. Late Uruk–Jemdet Nasr periods. Southern Mesopotamia</figcaption></figure>
<p>Outside Uruk, few sites in southern Mesopotamia have yielded levels contemporary with the Uruk period. Soundages carried out on the sites of most of the key cities of Mesopotamia in the historic period have revealed that they were occupied in this period (<a href="/wiki/Kish_(Sumer)" title="Kish (Sumer)">Kish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Girsu" title="Girsu">Girsu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nippur" title="Nippur">Nippur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ur" title="Ur">Ur</a>, perhaps <a href="/wiki/Shuruppak" title="Shuruppak">Shuruppak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Larsa" title="Larsa">Larsa</a>, and further north in <a href="/wiki/Diyala_Governorate" title="Diyala Governorate">Diyala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tell_Asmar" class="mw-redirect" title="Tell Asmar">Tell Asmar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Khafajah" title="Khafajah">Khafajah</a>). The sacred quarter of <a href="/wiki/Eridu" title="Eridu">Eridu</a>, site of the main monumental structures of the Ubaid period in Lower Mesopotamia, is poorly known for the Uruk period though Uruk Period pottery was found there.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The only important structure from the end of the 4th millennium BC so far known from the region outside Uruk is the 'Painted Temple' on the platform of <a href="/wiki/Tell_Uqair" title="Tell Uqair">Tell Uqair</a>, which dates to the end of the Uruk period or perhaps the Jemdet Nasr period, and consists of two terraces superimposed on one another with a building of around 18 x 22 m identified as having a cultic function.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> More recently, a level belonging to the Uruk period has been revealed on the tell southeast of the site of <a href="/wiki/Abu_Salabikh" title="Abu Salabikh">Abu Salabikh</a> ('Uruk Mound'), covering only 10 hectares.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This site was surrounded by a wall which has been only partially revealed and several buildings have been brought to light, including a platform which supported a building, only traces of which remain. As for the site of <a href="/wiki/Jemdet_Nasr" title="Jemdet Nasr">Jemdet Nasr</a>, which has given its name to the period of transition from the Uruk period to the Early Dynastic period, it is divided into two main tells and it is on the second (<i>Mound B</i>) that the most important building has been brought to light, which contained a substantial cache of administrative documents—more than 200 tablets with impressions of cylinder seals.<sup id="cite_ref-matthews-55/4-196-203_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-matthews-55/4-196-203-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Neighbouring_regions">Neighbouring regions</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uruk_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Neighbouring regions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The sources relating to the Uruk period derive from a group of sites distributed over an immense area, covering all of <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> and the neighbouring regions up to central <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> and southeastern <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>. The Uruk culture itself is certainly characterised mainly by sites of southern Mesopotamia and others which seem to have directly resulted from migrations from this region (the 'colonies' or 'emporia'), which are clearly part of the Uruk culture. But the phenomenon which is known as the Uruk expansion is detected on sites situated across a vast zone of influence, covering the whole Near East, regions which were not all really part of the Uruk culture, which was strictly-speaking limited to Lower Mesopotamia. The relations of some areas with the Uruk culture are very unclear, such as the little-known cultures of the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> in this period, and Egypt whose exact relations with the Uruk culture were distant and are the object of debate, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, where the influence of southern Mesopotamia remains barely perceptible. But in other areas the Uruk culture is more evident, such as Upper Mesopotamia, northern Syria, western Iran and southeastern Anatolia. They generally experienced an evolution similar to that of lower Mesopotamia, with the development of urban agglomerations and larger political entities and they were strongly influenced by the culture of the 'centre' in the later part of the period (c. 3400–3200), before a general strengthening of their own regional cultures took place at the turn of the 3rd millennium BC. The interpretation of the expansion of the Uruk culture into neighbouring regions poses numerous problems and many explanatory models (general and regional) have been proposed in order to explain it.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Susiana_and_the_Iranian_Plateau">Susiana and the Iranian Plateau</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uruk_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Susiana and the Iranian Plateau"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1237032888/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:292px;max-width:292px"><div class="trow"><div class="theader">Accounting tokens</div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:106px;max-width:106px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:189px;overflow:hidden"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Accountancy_clay_envelope_Louvre_Sb1932.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Accountancy_clay_envelope_Louvre_Sb1932.jpg/104px-Accountancy_clay_envelope_Louvre_Sb1932.jpg" decoding="async" width="104" height="189" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Accountancy_clay_envelope_Louvre_Sb1932.jpg/156px-Accountancy_clay_envelope_Louvre_Sb1932.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Accountancy_clay_envelope_Louvre_Sb1932.jpg/208px-Accountancy_clay_envelope_Louvre_Sb1932.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1100" data-file-height="2000" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">Clay envelope and its tokens. <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a>, Uruk period</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:189px;overflow:hidden"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Clay_accounting_tokens_Susa_Louvre_n2.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Clay_accounting_tokens_Susa_Louvre_n2.jpg/180px-Clay_accounting_tokens_Susa_Louvre_n2.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="189" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Clay_accounting_tokens_Susa_Louvre_n2.jpg/270px-Clay_accounting_tokens_Susa_Louvre_n2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Clay_accounting_tokens_Susa_Louvre_n2.jpg/360px-Clay_accounting_tokens_Susa_Louvre_n2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2450" data-file-height="2575" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">Clay accounting tokens. <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a>, Uruk period</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The region around <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a> in the southwest of modern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, is located right next to lower Mesopotamia, which exercised a powerful influence on it from the 5th millennium BC, and might be considered to have been part of the Uruk culture in the second half of the 4th millennium BC, either as a result of conquest or a more gradual acculturation, but it did retain its own unique characteristics.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Uruk period levels at Susa are called Susa I (c. 4000–3700 BC) and Susa II (c. 3700–3100 BC), during which the site became an urban settlement. Susa I saw the beginning of monumental architecture on the site, with the construction of a 'High Terrace', which was increased during Susa II to measure roughly 60 x 45 metres. The most interesting aspect of this site is the objects discovered there, which are the most important evidence available to us for the art of the Uruk period and the beginning of administration and writing. The <a href="/wiki/Cylinder_seal" title="Cylinder seal">cylinder seals</a> of Susa I and Susa II have a very rich iconography, uniquely emphasising scenes of everyday life, although there is also some kind of local potentate which P. Amiet sees as a 'proto-royal figure,' preceding the 'priest-kings' of Late Uruk.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These cylinder seals, as well as <a href="/wiki/Bulla_(seal)" title="Bulla (seal)">bullae</a> and clay tokens, indicate the rise of administration and of accounting techniques at Susa during the second half of the 4th millennium BC. Susa has also yielded some of the most ancient writing tablets, making it a key site for our understanding of the origins of writing. Other sites in Susiana also have archaeological levels belonging to this period, like <a href="/wiki/Jafarabad,_Alborz" title="Jafarabad, Alborz">Jaffarabad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chogha_Mish" title="Chogha Mish">Chogha Mish</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Further north, in the <a href="/wiki/Zagros" class="mw-redirect" title="Zagros">Zagros</a>, the site of <a href="/wiki/Godin_Tepe" title="Godin Tepe">Godin Tepe</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Kangavar" title="Kangavar">Kangavar</a> valley is particularly important. Level V of this site belongs to the Uruk period. Remains have been uncovered of an ovoid wall, enclosing several buildings organised around a central court, with a large structure to the north which might be a public building. The material culture has some traits which are shared with that of Late Uruk and Susa II. Level V of Godin Tepe could be interpreted as an establishment of merchants from Susa and/or lower Mesopotamia, interested in the location of the site on commercial routes, especially those linked to the <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lapis_lazuli" title="Lapis lazuli">lapis lazuli</a> mines on the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Plateau" class="mw-redirect" title="Iranian Plateau">Iranian Plateau</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Further east, the key site of <a href="/wiki/Tepe_Sialk" title="Tepe Sialk">Tepe Sialk</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Kashan" title="Kashan">Kashan</a>, shows no clear evidence of links with the Uruk culture in its Level III, but <a href="/wiki/Beveled_rim_bowl" title="Beveled rim bowl">beveled rim bowls</a> are found all the way out to Tepe Ghabristan in the <a href="/wiki/Elbourz" class="mw-redirect" title="Elbourz">Elbourz</a><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and at <a href="/wiki/Konar_Sandal#Mahtoutabad" title="Konar Sandal">Mahtoutabad</a> further to the southeast.
</p><p>In this region, the retreat of the Uruk culture resulted in a particular phenomenon, the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Elamite" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Elamite">Proto-Elamite</a> civilization, which seems to have been centred on the region of <a href="/wiki/Anshan_(Persia)" title="Anshan (Persia)">Tell-e Malyan</a> and Susiana and seems to have taken over the Uruk culture's links with the Iranian plateau.<sup id="cite_ref-butiran_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-butiran-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Upper_Mesopotamia_and_northern_Syria">Upper Mesopotamia and northern Syria</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uruk_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Upper Mesopotamia and northern Syria"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Several important sites of the Uruk period have been excavated in the Middle <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a> region, during the salvage campaigns preceding the construction of hydroelectric dams in the area.<sup id="cite_ref-huot8993_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-huot8993-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is largely as a result of the findings of these excavations that ideas of an "Uruk expansion" have arisen.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Habuba_Kabira">Habuba Kabira</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uruk_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Habuba Kabira"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The best known site is <a href="/wiki/Habuba_Kabira" title="Habuba Kabira">Habuba Kabira</a>, a fortified port on the right bank of the river in Syria. The city covered around 22 hectares, surrounded by a defensive wall, roughly 10 percent of which has been uncovered. Study of the buildings on this site shows that it was a planned settlement, which would have required significant means. The archaeological material from the site is identical to that of Uruk, consisting of pottery, cylinder-seals, bullae, accounting <i>calculi</i>, and numerical tablets from the end of the period. Thus this new city has every appearance of being an Urukian colony. Around 20 residences of various sorts have been excavated. They have a tripartite plan, arranged around a reception hall with a foyer opening onto an internal courtyard, with additional rooms arranged around it. In the south of the site is a hill, Tell Qanas, which has a monumental group of several structures identified speculatively as 'temples' on an artificial terrace. The site was abandoned at the end of the 4th millennium BC, apparently without violence, during the period when the Uruk culture retreated.<sup id="cite_ref-strommenger_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-strommenger-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Habuba Kabira is similar in many ways to the nearby site of <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Aruda" title="Jebel Aruda">Jebel Aruda</a> on a rocky outcrop, only 8&#160;km further north. As at Habuba Kabira, there is an urban centre made up of residences of various kinds and a central monumental complex of two 'temples'. It is beyond doubt that this city too was built by 'Urukians'. A little further north, further Urukian outposts, <a href="/wiki/Tell_Qraya" class="mw-redirect" title="Tell Qraya">Tell Qraya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tell_Sheikh_Hassan" class="mw-redirect" title="Tell Sheikh Hassan">Tell Sheikh Hassan</a>, lay on the middle Euphrates. It is possible that these sites were part of a state implanted in the region by people from south Mesopotamia and were developed in order to take advantage of important commercial routes.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Tell_Brak">Tell Brak</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uruk_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Tell Brak"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:TellBrak-NE.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/TellBrak-NE.jpg/220px-TellBrak-NE.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/TellBrak-NE.jpg/330px-TellBrak-NE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/TellBrak-NE.jpg/440px-TellBrak-NE.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="1285" /></a><figcaption>Ruins of <a href="/wiki/Tell_Brak" title="Tell Brak">Tell Brak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tell_Brak" title="Tell Brak">Tell Brak</a></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Khabur_(Euphrates)" title="Khabur (Euphrates)">Khabur</a> valley, <a href="/wiki/Tell_Brak" title="Tell Brak">Tell Brak</a> was an important urban centre from the 5th millennium BC, one of the largest of the Uruk period, since it covered over 110 hectares at its height. Some residences from the period have been uncovered, along with pottery typical of Uruk, but what has received the most attention is a succession of monuments which are definitely for cultic purposes. The 'Eye Temple' (as its final stage is known) has walls decorated with terracotta cones which form a mosaic and with inlays of coloured stones and a platform which might have been an altar and is decorated with gold leaf, lapis lazuli, silver nails, and white marble in a central T-shaped room. The most remarkable find are over two hundred "eye figurines" which give the building its name. These figurines have enormous eyes and are definitely votive deposits. Tell Brak has also produced evidence of writing: a numeric tablet and two <a href="/wiki/Pictographic" class="mw-redirect" title="Pictographic">pictographic</a> tablets showing some unique features in comparison to those of southern Mesopotamia, which indicates that there was a distinct local tradition of writing.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A little to the east of Tell Brak is <a href="/wiki/Hamoukar" title="Hamoukar">Hamoukar</a>, where excavations began in 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This vast site has provided the normal evidence found at sites under Urukian influence in Upper Mesopotamia (pottery, seals) and evidence of the existence of an important urban centre in this region in the Uruk period, like Tell Brak. Further to the east again, the site of <a href="/wiki/Tell_al-Hawa" title="Tell al-Hawa">Tell al-Hawa</a>, Iraq also shows evidence of contacts with lower Mesopotamia.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Tell_Kuyunjik">Tell Kuyunjik</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uruk_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Tell Kuyunjik"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>On the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a>, the site of <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">Nineveh</a> (Tell Kuyunjik, level 4) was located on some major commercial routes and was also within the Urukian sphere of influence. The site covered roughly 40 hectares—the whole area of Tell Kuyunjik. The material remains of the period are very limited, but beveled rim bowls, an accounting bulla, and a numerical tablet characteristic of the Late Uruk period have been found.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Nearby, <a href="/wiki/Tepe_Gawra" title="Tepe Gawra">Tepe Gawra</a>, which was also important in the Ubayd period, is an important case of the changing scale of monumental architecture and of political entities between the end of the 5th millennium and the first half of the 4th millennium BC (Level XII to VIII). The excavations there have revealed some very rich tombs, different kinds of residence, workshops, and very large buildings with an official or religious function (notably the 'round structure'), which may indicate that Tepe Gawra was a regional political centre. However, it declined before the Uruk expansion into Upper Mesopotamia.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Southeast_Anatolia">Southeast Anatolia</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uruk_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Southeast Anatolia"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Several sites have been excavated in the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a> valley in the south east of Anatolia, near the region of the Urukian sites of the middle Euphrates.<sup id="cite_ref-huot8993_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-huot8993-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hac%C4%B1nebi_Tepe" title="Hacınebi Tepe">Hacınebi Tepe</a>, near modern <a href="/wiki/Birecik" title="Birecik">Birecik</a> in <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Eanl%C4%B1urfa" class="mw-redirect" title="Şanlıurfa">Şanlıurfa</a>, was excavated by G. Stein and was located at the crossroads of some important commercial routes. Beveled rim bowls appear from phase B1 (c. 3800/3700 BC) and they are also present in phase B2 (3700–3300 BC), along with other objects characteristic of Late Uruk, like mosaics of clay cones, a terracotta sickle, an accounting bulla imprinted with the pattern from a cylinder seal, an uninscribed clay tablet, etc. This material co-exists with local pottery, which remains dominant throughout. The excavator of the site thinks that there was an enclave of people from Lower Mesopotamia who lived on the site alongside a majority population of local people.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Other sites have been excavated in the region of <a href="/wiki/Samsat" title="Samsat">Samsat</a> (also in the Euphrates valley). An Urukian site was revealed at Samsat during a hasty rescue excavation before the area was flooded as a result of the construction of a hydroelectric dam. Fragments of clay cones from a wall mosaic were found. A little to the south is Kurban Höyük, where clay cones and pottery characteristic of Uruk have also been found in tripartite buildings.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Further to the north, the site of <a href="/wiki/Arslantepe" title="Arslantepe">Arslantepe</a>, located in the suburbs of <a href="/wiki/Malatya" title="Malatya">Malatya</a>, is the most remarkable site of the period in eastern Anatolia. It has been excavated by M. Frangipane. During the first half of the 4th millennium BC, this site was dominated by a building called 'Temple C' by the excavators, which was built on a platform. It was abandoned around 3500 BC and replaced by a monumental complex which seems to have been the regional centre of power. The culture of Late Uruk had a discernible influence, which can be seen most clearly in the numerous sealings found on the site, many of which are in a south Mesopotamian style. Around 3000 BC, the site was destroyed by a fire. The monuments were not restored and the <a href="/wiki/Kura%E2%80%93Araxes_culture" title="Kura–Araxes culture">Kura–Araxes culture</a> centred on the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Caucasus" class="mw-redirect" title="Southern Caucasus">southern Caucasus</a> became the dominant material culture on the site.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Further west, the site of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tepecik_%C3%87iftlik&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Tepecik Çiftlik (page does not exist)">Tepecik</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">&#160;&#91;<a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tepecik-%C3%87iftlik" class="extiw" title="de:Tepecik-Çiftlik">de</a>; <a href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tepecik" class="extiw" title="fr:Tepecik">fr</a>; <a href="https://tr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tepecik_%E2%80%93_%C3%87iftlik_H%C3%B6y%C3%BC%C4%9F%C3%BC" class="extiw" title="tr:Tepecik – Çiftlik Höyüğü">tr</a>&#93;</span> near <a href="/wiki/%C3%87iftlik,_Ni%C4%9Fde" title="Çiftlik, Niğde">Çiftlik, Niğde</a> has also revealed pottery influenced by that of Uruk.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> But in this region, the Urukian influence becomes increasingly ephemeral, as one gets further from Mesopotamia.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Uruk_expansion">Uruk expansion</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uruk_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Uruk expansion"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Uruk_expansion.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Uruk_expansion.svg/400px-Uruk_expansion.svg.png" decoding="async" width="400" height="324" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Uruk_expansion.svg/600px-Uruk_expansion.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Uruk_expansion.svg/800px-Uruk_expansion.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1185" data-file-height="961" /></a><figcaption>The 'Uruk expansion': sites representing the 'centre' and 'periphery'. Tell Sheikh Hassan settlement can be seen on this map to the upper left.</figcaption></figure>
<p>After the discovery in Syria of the sites at <a href="/wiki/Habuba_Kabira" title="Habuba Kabira">Habuba Kabira</a> (see above) and <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Aruda" title="Jebel Aruda">Jebel Aruda</a> in the 1970s, they were identified as colonies or trading posts of the Uruk civilisation settled far from their own lands. Indeed these two sites, along with the smaller site of <a href="/wiki/Tell_Sheikh_Hassan" class="mw-redirect" title="Tell Sheikh Hassan">Tell Sheikh Hassan</a>, feature no significant preexisting occupation, and are in fact all located in the same geographical area at a significant river ford along the Middle Euphrates.<sup id="cite_ref-Algaze_2013_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Algaze_2013-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Tell Sheikh Hassan was located on the left (eastern) bank of the river, and it was founded during the Middle Uruk period. Later, during the earlier part of the Late Uruk period, Jebel Aruda, and Habuba Kabira-South, together with Tell Qanas right next to it, were founded on the opposite bank of the river.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Together the last three comprised a much larger urban enclave (about 20–40 ha in extent) compared to Sheikh Hassan.<sup id="cite_ref-Algaze_2013_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Algaze_2013-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Later, questions arose about the relationship between Lower Mesopotamia and the neighbouring regions. The fact that the characteristics of the culture of the Uruk region are found across such a large territory (from northern Syria to the Iranian plateau), with Lower Mesopotamia as a clear centre, led the archaeologists who studied this period to see this phenomenon as an 'Uruk expansion'.
</p><p>Recent excavations have focused on sites outside Mesopotamia, as a 'periphery', and with an interest in how they related to the 'centre', the site of Uruk. Subsequently, theories and knowledge have developed to the point of general models, drawing on parallels from other places and periods, which has posed some problems in terms of getting the models and parallels to fit the facts revealed by excavations.<sup id="cite_ref-histo_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-histo-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The main issue here is how to interpret the word ‘expansion’. Nobody really doubts that, for many centuries, there was a significant cultural influence of Uruk in the wide areas north and east of it. But was it really a <i>political takeover</i> of an area, which constitutes the more extreme colonization hypothesis? Or was it perhaps some sort of an infiltration by groups of Urukean or southern Mesopotamian people trying to farm suitable lands – perhaps even by some refugees fleeing growing political oppression and overcrowding at Uruk?<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Another hypothesis is perhaps the need to control valuable trading networks, and setting up the type of <a href="/wiki/Karum_(trade_post)" title="Karum (trade post)">Karum</a> trading posts, which was done during an Old Assyrian period. These types of strategies did not involve the state authorities, as such, but was done by commercial trading houses.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Guillermo_Algaze" title="Guillermo Algaze">Guillermo Algaze</a> adopted the <a href="/wiki/World-systems_theory" title="World-systems theory">World-systems theory</a> of <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Wallerstein" title="Immanuel Wallerstein">Immanuel Wallerstein</a> and theories of <a href="/wiki/International_trade" title="International trade">international trade</a>, elaborating the model colonialism and incipient imperial expansion that sought to explain the Uruk civilization.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In his view, which has met with some approval, but has also found many critics,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  the 'Urukians' created a collection of colonies outside Lower Mesopotamia, first in Upper Mesopotamia (Habuba Kabira and Jebel Aruda, as well as Nineveh, Tell Brak and Samsat to the north), then in Susiana and the Iranian plateau. For Algaze, the motivation of this activity is considered to be a form of economic imperialism: the elites of southern Mesopotamia wanted to obtain the numerous raw materials which were not available in the Tigris and Euphrates floodplains, and founded their colonies on nodal points which controlled a vast commercial network (although it remains impossible to determine what exactly was exchanged), settling them with refugees as in some models of <a href="/wiki/Greek_colonisation" title="Greek colonisation">Greek colonisation</a>. The relations established between Lower Mesopotamia and the neighbouring regions were thus of an asymmetric kind. The inhabitants of Lower Mesopotamia had the advantage in the interactions with neighbouring regions as a result of the high productivity of their lands, which had allowed their region to "take off" (he speaks of "the Sumerian takeoff") resulting in both a <a href="/wiki/Comparative_advantage" title="Comparative advantage">comparative advantage</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Competitive_advantage" title="Competitive advantage">competitive advantage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They had the most developed state structures and were thus able to develop long-distance commercial links, exercise influence over their neighbours, and perhaps engage in military conquest.
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1237032888/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:442px;max-width:442px"><div class="trow"><div class="theader">Susa King-priest</div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:121px;max-width:121px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:138px;overflow:hidden"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:King-priest_with_bow_fighting_enemies,_with_horned_temple_(composite_of_two_imprints_of_the_same_cylinder_seal).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/King-priest_with_bow_fighting_enemies%2C_with_horned_temple_%28composite_of_two_imprints_of_the_same_cylinder_seal%29.jpg/119px-King-priest_with_bow_fighting_enemies%2C_with_horned_temple_%28composite_of_two_imprints_of_the_same_cylinder_seal%29.jpg"
