2x" data-file-width="3744" data-file-height="4357" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">Imprints</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:317px;max-width:317px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:138px;overflow:hidden"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:King-priest_with_bow_fighting_enemies,_with_horned_temple_(reconstitution).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/King-priest_with_bow_fighting_enemies%2C_with_horned_temple_%28reconstitution%29.jpg/315px-King-priest_with_bow_fighting_enemies%2C_with_horned_temple_%28reconstitution%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="315" height="139" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/King-priest_with_bow_fighting_enemies%2C_with_horned_temple_%28reconstitution%29.jpg/473px-King-priest_with_bow_fighting_enemies%2C_with_horned_temple_%28reconstitution%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/King-priest_with_bow_fighting_enemies%2C_with_horned_temple_%28reconstitution%29.jpg/630px-King-priest_with_bow_fighting_enemies%2C_with_horned_temple_%28reconstitution%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2319" data-file-height="1024" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption text-align-center">Reconstitution</div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">King-priest with bow fighting enemies, with horned temple to the right. <a href="/wiki/Susa_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Susa II">Susa II</a> or Uruk period (3800-3100 BCE), found in excavations at <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a>. Louvre Museum.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></div></div></div></div>
<p>Algaze's theory, like other alternative models, has been criticised, particularly because a solid model remains difficult to demonstrate while the Uruk civilization remains poorly known in Lower Mesopotamia aside from the two monumental complexes that have been excavated at Uruk itself. We are therefore poorly placed to evaluate the impact of the development of southern Mesopotamia, since we have almost no archaeological evidence about it. Moreover, the chronology of this period is far from established, which makes it difficult to date the expansion. It has proven difficult to make the levels at different sites correspond closely enough to attribute them to a single period, making the elaboration of relative chronology very complicated. Among the theories that have been advanced to explain the Uruk expansion, the commercial explanation is frequently revived. However, although long-distance trade is undoubtedly a secondary phenomenon for the south Mesopotamian states compared to local production and seems to follow the development of increased social complexity rather than causing it, this does not necessarily prove a process of colonisation.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some other theories propose a form of agrarian colonisation resulting from a shortage of land in Lower Mesopotamia or a migration of refugees after the Uruk region suffered ecological or political upheavals. These explanations are largely advanced to explain the sites of the Syro-Anatolian world, rather than as global theories.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Other explanations avoid political and economic factors in order to focus on the Uruk expansion as a long term cultural phenomenon, using concepts of <a href="/wiki/Koine" class="mw-redirect" title="Koine">koine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acculturation" title="Acculturation">acculturation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hybridity" title="Hybridity">hybridity</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Cultural_emulation&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Cultural emulation (page does not exist)">cultural emulation</a> to emphasise their differentiation according to the cultural regions and sites in question. P. Butterlin has proposed that the links tying southern Mesopotamia to its neighbours in this period should be seen as a 'world culture' rather than an economic 'world system', in which the Uruk region provided a model to its neighbours, each of which took up more adaptable elements in their own way and retained some local traits essentially unchanged. This is intended to explain the different degrees of influence or acculturation.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In effect, the impact of Uruk is generally distinguished in specific sites and regions, which has led to the development of multiple typologies of material considered to be characteristic of the Uruk culture (especially the pottery and the beveled rim bowls). It has been possible to identify multiple types of site, ranging from colonies that could be actual Urukian sites through to trading posts with an Urukian enclave and sites that are mostly local with a weak or non-existent Urukian influence, as well as others where contacts are more or less strong without supplanting the local culture.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The case of Susiana and the Iranian plateau, which is generally studied by different scholars from those who work on Syrian and Anatolian sites, has led to some attempted explanations based on local developments, notably the development of the proto-Elamite culture, which is sometimes seen as a product of the expansion and sometimes as an adversary.<sup id="cite_ref-butiran_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-butiran-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The case of the southern Levant and Egypt is different again and helps to highlight the role of local cultures as receivers of the Uruk culture.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the Levant there was no stratified society with embryonic cities and bureaucracy, and therefore no strong elite to act as local intermediaries of Urukian culture and as a result Urukian influence is especially weak.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In Egypt, Urukian influence seems to be limited to a few objects which were seen as prestigious or exotic (most notably the knife of Jebel el-Arak), chosen by the elite at a moment when they needed to assert their power in a developing state.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Mesopotamia-Egypt_trade_routes.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Mesopotamia-Egypt_trade_routes.jpg/330px-Mesopotamia-Egypt_trade_routes.jpg" decoding="async" width="330" height="222" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Mesopotamia-Egypt_trade_routes.jpg/495px-Mesopotamia-Egypt_trade_routes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Mesopotamia-Egypt_trade_routes.jpg/660px-Mesopotamia-Egypt_trade_routes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1364" data-file-height="916" /></a><figcaption>Possible Mesopotamia-Egypt trade routes from the 4th millennium BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-Redford_22_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Redford_22-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MKH427_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MKH427-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>It might be added that an interpretation of the relations of this period as centre/periphery interaction, although often relevant in period, risks prejudicing researchers to see decisions in an asymmetric or diffusionist fashion, and this needs to be nuanced. Thus, it increasingly appears that the regions neighbouring Lower Mesopotamia did not wait for the Urukians in order to begin an advanced process of increasing social complexity or urbanisation, as the example of the large site of <a href="/wiki/Tell_Brak" title="Tell Brak">Tell Brak</a> in Syria shows, which encourages us to imagine the phenomenon from a more 'symmetrical' angle.<sup id="cite_ref-buturb_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buturb-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-brakurb_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brakurb-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Indeed, at Tell Brak, we find that this city developed as an urban center slightly earlier than the better known cities of southern Mesopotamia, such as Uruk.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Egypt">Egypt</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uruk_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Egypt"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Egypt-Mesopotamia_relations" class="mw-redirect" title="Egypt-Mesopotamia relations">Egypt-Mesopotamia relations</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Egypt-Mesopotamia_relations" class="mw-redirect" title="Egypt-Mesopotamia relations">Egypt-Mesopotamia relations</a> seem to have developed from the 4th millennium BCE, starting in the Uruk period for <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> and in the pre-literate <a href="/wiki/Gerzean_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Gerzean culture">Gerzean culture</a> for <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Egypt" title="Prehistoric Egypt">Prehistoric Egypt</a> (circa 3500-3200 BCE).<sup id="cite_ref-Shaw,_Ian_1995_p._109_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw,_Ian_1995_p._109-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mitchell_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Influences can be seen in the visual arts of Egypt, in imported products, and also in the possible transfer of writing from Mesopotamia to Egypt,<sup id="cite_ref-Mitchell_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and generated "deep-seated" parallels in the early stages of both cultures.<sup id="cite_ref-MKH427_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MKH427-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uruk_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Society and culture"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Uruk3000BCE.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Uruk3000BCE.jpg/330px-Uruk3000BCE.jpg" decoding="async" width="330" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Uruk3000BCE.jpg/495px-Uruk3000BCE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Uruk3000BCE.jpg/660px-Uruk3000BCE.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1650" data-file-height="826" /></a><figcaption>Modern clay impression of a cylinder seal with monstrous lions and lion-headed eagles, Mesopotamia, Uruk Period (4100 BC–3000 BC). Louvre Museum.</figcaption></figure>
<p>On the cusp of prehistory and history, the Uruk period can be considered 'revolutionary' and foundational in many ways. Many of the innovations which it produced were turning points in the history of Mesopotamia and indeed of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is in this period that one sees the general appearance of the <a href="/wiki/Potter%27s_wheel" title="Potter&#39;s wheel">potter's wheel</a>, writing, the city, and the state. There is new progress in the development of state-societies, such that specialists see fit to label them as 'complex' (in comparison with earlier societies which are said to be 'simple').
</p><p>Scholarship is therefore interested in this period as a crucial step in the evolution of society—a long and cumulative process whose roots could be seen at the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> more than 6000 years earlier and which had picked up steam in the preceding Ubayd period in Mesopotamia. This is especially the case in English-language scholarship, in which the theoretical approaches have been largely inspired by anthropology since the 1970s, and which has studied the Uruk period from the angle of 'complexity' in analysing the appearance of early states, an expanding social hierarchy, intensification of long-distance trade, etc.<sup id="cite_ref-histo_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-histo-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In order to discern the key developments which make this period a crucial step in the history of the ancient Near East, research focusses mainly on the centre, Lower Mesopotamia, and on sites in neighbouring regions which are clearly integrated into the civilization which originated there (especially the 'colonies' of the middle Euphrates). The aspects traced here are mostly those of the Late Uruk period, which is the best known and undoubtedly the period in which the most rapid change took place—it is the moment when the characteristic traits of the ancient Mesopotamian civilization were established.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Technology_and_economy">Technology and economy</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uruk_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Technology and economy"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The 4th millennium BC saw the appearance of new tools which had a substantial impact on the societies that used them, especially in the economic sphere. Some of them, although known in the preceding period, only came into use on a large scale at this time. The use of these inventions produced economic and social changes in combination with the emergence of political structures and administrative states.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Agriculture_and_pastoralism">Agriculture and pastoralism</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uruk_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Agriculture and pastoralism"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_in_ancient_Mesopotamia" class="mw-redirect" title="Agriculture in ancient Mesopotamia">Agriculture in ancient Mesopotamia</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Cylinder_seal_cowshed_Louvre_Klq17.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Cylinder_seal_cowshed_Louvre_Klq17.jpg/330px-Cylinder_seal_cowshed_Louvre_Klq17.jpg" decoding="async" width="330" height="120" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Cylinder_seal_cowshed_Louvre_Klq17.jpg/495px-Cylinder_seal_cowshed_Louvre_Klq17.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Cylinder_seal_cowshed_Louvre_Klq17.jpg/660px-Cylinder_seal_cowshed_Louvre_Klq17.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2200" data-file-height="800" /></a><figcaption>Cylinder seal and impression: cattle herd at the cowshed. White limestone, Mesopotamia, Uruk Period (4100 BC–3000 BC).</figcaption></figure>
<p>In the agricultural sphere, several important innovations were made between the end of the Ubayd period and the Uruk period, which have been referred to in total as the 'Second Agricultural Revolution' (the first being the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a>). A first group of developments took place in the field of cereal cultivation, followed by the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Ard_(plough)" title="Ard (plough)">ard</a>—a wooden plough pulled by an animal (ass or ox)—towards the end of the 4th millennium BC, which enabled the production of long furrows in the earth.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This made the agricultural work in the sowing season much simpler than previously, when this work had to be done by hand with tools like the <a href="/wiki/Hoe_(tool)" title="Hoe (tool)">hoe</a>. The harvest was made easier after the Ubayd period by the widespread adoption of terracotta <a href="/wiki/Sickle" title="Sickle">sickles</a>. <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">Irrigation</a> techniques also seem to have improved in the Uruk period. These different inventions allowed the progressive development of a new agricultural landscape, characteristic of ancient Lower Mesopotamia. It consisted of long rectangular fields suited for being worked in furrows, each bordered by a little irrigation channel. According to M. Liverani, these replaced the earlier basins irrigated laboriously by hand.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As for the <a href="/wiki/Date_palm" title="Date palm">date palm</a>, we know from archaeological discoveries that these fruits are consumed in Lower Mesopotamia in the 5th millennium BC. The date of its first cultivation by man can't be precisely determined: it is commonly supposed that the culture of this tree knew its development during the Late Uruk period, but the texts are not explicit on this matter.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This system which progressively developed over two thousand years enabled higher yields, leaving more surplus than previously for workers, whose rations mainly consisted of barley.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The human, material, and technical resources were now available for agriculture based on paid labour, although family-based farming remained the base unit. All of this undoubtedly led to population increase and thus urbanisation and the development of state structures.<sup id="cite_ref-livdemo_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-livdemo-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The Uruk period also saw important developments in the realm of pastoralism. First of all, it is in this period that the wild <a href="/wiki/Onager" title="Onager">onager</a> was finally <a href="/wiki/Domesticated" class="mw-redirect" title="Domesticated">domesticated</a> as the donkey. It was the first domesticated <a href="/wiki/Equid" class="mw-redirect" title="Equid">equid</a> in the region and became the most important <a href="/wiki/Beast_of_burden" class="mw-redirect" title="Beast of burden">beast of burden</a> in the Near East (the <a href="/wiki/Dromedary" title="Dromedary">dromedary</a> was only domesticated in the 3rd millennium BC, in <a href="/wiki/Arabia" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabia">Arabia</a>). With its high transport capacity (about double that of a human), it enabled the further development of trade over short and long distances.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ane_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ane-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Pastoralism of animals which had already been domesticated (sheep, horses, cattle) also developed further. Previously these animals had been raised mainly as sources of meat, but they now became more important for the products which they provided (wool, fur, hides, milk) and as beasts of burden.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This final aspect was especially connected with the cattle, which became essential for work in the fields with the appearance of the ard, and the donkey which assumed a major role in the transportation of goods.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-center" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Uruk_Trough.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Uruk_Trough.jpg/550px-Uruk_Trough.jpg" decoding="async" width="550" height="173" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Uruk_Trough.jpg/825px-Uruk_Trough.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Uruk_Trough.jpg/1100px-Uruk_Trough.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4695" data-file-height="1479" /></a><figcaption>The <a href="/wiki/Uruk_Trough" title="Uruk Trough">Uruk Trough</a>, showing cattle and a stable. Circa 3300-3000 BC, British Museum</figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Crafts_and_construction">Crafts and construction</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uruk_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Crafts and construction"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The development of <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">woolworking</a>, which increasingly replaced <a href="/wiki/Linen" title="Linen">linen</a> in the production of textiles, had important economic implications. Beyond the expansion of <a href="/wiki/Sheep_farming" title="Sheep farming">sheep farming</a>, these were notably in the institutional framework,<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which led to changes in agricultural practice with the introduction of pasturage for these animals in the fields, as <a href="/wiki/Convertible_husbandry" title="Convertible husbandry">convertible husbandry</a>, and in the hilly and mountainous zones around Mesopotamia (following a kind of <a href="/wiki/Transhumance" title="Transhumance">transhumance</a>). The relative decline in the cultivation of <a href="/wiki/Flax" title="Flax">flax</a> for linen freed land for the growth of cereals as well as <a href="/wiki/Sesame" title="Sesame">sesame</a>, which was introduced to Lower Mesopotamia at this time and was a profitable replacement for flax since it provided <a href="/wiki/Sesame_oil" title="Sesame oil">sesame oil</a>. Subsequently, this resulted in the development of an important textile industry, attested by many cylinder-seal impressions. This too was largely an institutional development, since wool became an essential element in the maintenance rations provided to workers along with barley. The establishment of this 'wool cycle' alongside the 'barley cycle' (the terms used by <a href="/wiki/Mario_Liverani" title="Mario Liverani">Mario Liverani</a>) had the same results for the processing and its redistribution, giving the ancient Mesopotamian economy its two key industries and went along with the economic development of large systems. Moreover, wool could be exported easily (unlike perishable food products), which may have meant that the Mesopotamians had something to exchange with their neighbours who had more in the way of primary materials.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading5"><h5 id="Pottery">Pottery</h5><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uruk_period&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Pottery"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Vorderasiatisches_Museum_Berlin_045.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Vorderasiatisches_Museum_Berlin_045.jpg/220px-Vorderasiatisches_Museum_Berlin_045.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Vorderasiatisches_Museum_Berlin_045.jpg/330px-Vorderasiatisches_Museum_Berlin_045.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Vorderasiatisches_Museum_Berlin_045.jpg/440px-Vorderasiatisches_Museum_Berlin_045.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="2304" /></a><figcaption>Pottery from the Late Uruk period: wheel-made pottery at right and bevelled rim bowls at left, Pergamon Museum.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The production of <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">pottery</a> was revolutionised by the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Potter%27s_wheel" title="Potter&#39;s wheel">potter's wheel</a> in the course of the 4th millennium, which was developed in two stages: first a slow wheel and then a rapid one. As a result of this it was no longer necessary to shape ceramics with the hands alone and the shaping process was more rapid.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Potters' <a href="/wiki/Kiln" title="Kiln">kilns</a> were also improved. Pottery was simply coated with <a href="/wiki/Slip_(ceramics)" title="Slip (ceramics)">slip</a> to smooth the surface and decoration became less and less complex until there was basically none. Painted pottery was then secondary and the rare examples of decoration are mainly incisions (lozenge patterns or grid lines). Archaeological sites from this period produce large quantities of pottery, showing that a new level of mass-production had been reached, for a larger population—especially in cities in contact with large administrative systems. They were mainly used for holding various kinds of agricultural production (barley, beer, dates, milk, etc.) and were thus pervasive in everyday life. This period marks the appearance of potters who specialised in the production of large quantities of pottery, which resulted in the emergence of specialised districts within communities. Although the quality was low, the diversity of shapes and sizes became more important than previously, with the diversification of the functions served by pottery. Not all the pottery of this period was produced on the potter's wheel: the most distinctive vessel of the Uruk period, the <a href="/wiki/Beveled_rim_bowl" title="Beveled rim bowl">beveled rim bowls</a>, were hand-moulded.<sup id="cite_ref-BRB_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BRB-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

			<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px; height: 230px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Vase_Telloh_Louvre_AO14342.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Vase. 			<div class="gallerytext">Vase. Terracotta, ca. 3500–2900 BC. From Telloh, ancient city of Girsu.</div>
		</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px; height: 230px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Glockentopf.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Uruk period beveled rim bowl from Habuba Kabira South (Syria), ca. 3400–3200.
